## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: solid mechanics (thermo-mechanical constitutive modeling of materials)
**Analysis**: The variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which strongly indicates a constitutive law in continuum mechanics/materials science. Typical relationships include elastic stress–strain with temperature-dependent modulus, plasticity/yield with thermal softening, and visco-/thermo-activated forms where sigma decreases with increasing T at fixed epsilon.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hooke’s law (isothermal linear elasticity): sigma = c0 * epsilon (small-strain elastic regime)
- Thermoelastic modulus linear-in-temperature: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic stiffness changes approximately linearly with T over a limited range)
- Thermoelastic modulus exponential softening: sigma = c0 * exp(-c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic softening with increasing T, polymer/metal at elevated T)
- Thermoelastic modulus reciprocal form: sigma = c0 * epsilon / (T + c1) (strong softening vs T; convenient empirical fit over a band of T)
- Nonlinear elastic polynomial (Landau-type expansion): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon^2 + c2 * epsilon^3 (moderate nonlinearity in elastic response)
- Temperature-dependent nonlinear elasticity: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon + (c2 + c3 * T) * epsilon^3 (captures both nonlinearity and thermal softening/stiffening)
- Ramberg–Osgood-type invertible form (stress as function of strain): sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1)^c2 (empirical elastic-plastic transition without explicit yield point)
- Power-law hardening with thermal softening prefactor: sigma = c0 * exp(-c1 * T) * (Abs(epsilon) + c2)^c3 (strain hardening with temperature-dependent strength)
- Saturating hyperelastic form: sigma = c0 * tanh(c1 * epsilon) * exp(-c2 * T) (strain-stiffening that plateaus; temperature reduces overall scale)
- Neo-Hookean-like (incompressible uniaxial, small set of invariants): sigma = c0 * (exp(c1 * epsilon) - exp(-c2 * epsilon)) * exp(-c3 * T) (captures asymmetric tension/compression and thermal softening)
- Yield-plus-hardening (piecewise smoothed with softplus-like approximation): sigma = c0 * log(1 + exp(c1 * (epsilon - c2))) * exp(-c3 * T) (mimics elastic-to-plastic onset at epsilon≈c2 with thermal softening)
- Thermally activated flow stress (Eyring-like stress scale): sigma = c0 + c1 * asinh(c2 * epsilon) + c3 * T (common in polymers/creep-inspired fits where stress relates to inverse-sinh of deformation measure)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Elastic regime identification: sigma proportional to epsilon (or nearly so) at small |epsilon| suggests a temperature-dependent modulus multiplying epsilon.
- Thermal softening: decreasing sigma with increasing T at fixed epsilon is often captured by exp(-c*T), 1/(T+c), or linear (c0+c1*T) prefactors on an elastic/plastic backbone.
- Elastic–plastic transition without explicit piecewise rules: smooth convex functions like log(1+exp(c*(epsilon-c0))) approximate a yield point while remaining differentiable for regression.
- Strain hardening: sigma increasing faster than linearly with epsilon is frequently represented by (Abs(epsilon)+c)^n or polynomial/cubic terms; temperature typically scales the amplitude downward.
- Tension–compression asymmetry: combinations like exp(c*epsilon)-exp(-c*epsilon) (odd in epsilon) can capture different responses for positive vs negative strain.
- Finite-strain effects: exponential-in-epsilon terms often indicate multiplicative kinematics (e.g., stretch = exp(epsilon)) and become relevant when |epsilon| is not infinitesimal.
- Saturation/limiting stress at large strain: tanh(c*epsilon) (or similar bounded nonlinearities) indicates stress approaching a plateau (e.g., network locking or phenomenological fit limits).
- Additive vs multiplicative temperature effects: additive c*T terms suggest thermal stress offsets (e.g., constrained thermal strain), whereas multiplicative factors suggest temperature-dependent strength/stiffness.
- Regularization near zero strain: using Abs(epsilon)+c (with small c) avoids singular slopes and reflects that many constitutive fits are insensitive to the sign near epsilon=0.

This simplifies to: `-0.6057*T + 0.6057*298.00 + epsilon*3.1474*exp(-(T-298)²)`

The GT has:
1. A linear term in T (no epsilon dependence)
2. A constant term (no epsilon or T dependence)
3. A term that is epsilon times a Gaussian in T

Key structural features:
- Linear in T (pure T term)
- Linear in epsilon multiplied by exp(-(T-298)²) — a Gaussian in T
- The epsilon dependence is purely linear (epsilon appears to first power only)
- The T dependence in the exponential term is Gaussian

Let me check each candidate for structural compatibility:

**Critical observation:** The GT contains `exp(-(T-c)²)`, which is a Gaussian function of T. This is a very specific basis function. Most candidates don't contain any exponential in T of this form.

Let me scan for candidates that could potentially produce a Gaussian in T:

- Candidates with `exp(T*c)`: 17, 19, 41, 42, 43, 44, 45, 48 — these give `exp(c*T)`, which is exponential, not Gaussian. Cannot match `exp(-T²)`.
- Candidates with `sin(T*c)`: 1, 3, 11, 13, 17, 25, 50 — sin is periodic, cannot match Gaussian.
- Candidates with `log(T*c+1)`: 11, 28, 30, 38, 48, 50 — logarithmic, cannot match.
- Candidates with rational functions of T: many — rational functions cannot match Gaussian.
- Candidates with `sqrt(T)`, `T^(3/2)`: 20 — power functions, cannot match.
- Candidates with `T^c`: some have `T**c1*c2` — power law, not Gaussian.

None of the candidates contain `exp(-T²)` or any form that could produce a Gaussian in T. The Gaussian `exp(-(T-298)²)` is a fundamentally different basis function from:
- `exp(c*T)` (exponential)
- `sin(c*T)` (trigonometric)
- `log(T)` (logarithmic)
- Rational functions of T
- Power functions of T

Since `exp(-(T-c)²)` cannot be expressed as any combination of the basis functions present in any candidate, **no candidate can match the GT**.

Let me verify this by checking every candidate systematically — none contain `exp(-T²)` or `exp(T²)` type terms. The only exponentials in T are of the form `exp(c*T)` (linear exponent), which is fundamentally different from `exp(-T²)` (quadratic exponent). These are linearly independent functions and cannot be equated.
